Āmidī's Two-Property Argument for the Definition of Nazar
Al-Āmidī presents his two-property argument for the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's defense of the definition of nazar as 'seeking knowledge' by positing two properties: providing the origin of conjecture and providing its predominance.

Core Idea
A definition can mention one property if the property is comprehensive.

In this passage, al-Āmidī responds to a previous objection by arguing that nazar has two properties: providing the origin of conjecture and providing its predominance. He claims that mentioning one property in the definition is sufficient, as it is not necessary to list all properties. This lesson presents Āmidī's defense and sets the stage for the commentator's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read Āmidī's response
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice that he asserts nazar has two specific properties.
2
Identify the two properties
The two properties are providing the origin of conjecture (aṣl al-ẓann) and providing its predominance (ghālibat al-ẓann). Understand what these mean.
3
Understand the argument
Āmidī argues that mentioning one property suffices because it is not obligatory to mention all properties. This is his justification for the definition of nazar as 'that by which knowledge is sought'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنه الآمدي بأن له ( أي النظر ) خاصتين إفادة ( أصل ) الظن وإفادة غلبته بأن يزداد رجحانه وقوته متقارباً إلى الجزم ) وقد اكتفى ) في تعريفه ( بذكر إحداهما ) يعني إحدى الخاصتين ) ولا يجب ذكر ( الكل ) أي كل خواصه في تعريفه ( وفيه نظر إذ يوجب ) جوابه هذا ( جواز القناعة بقوله : يطلب به علم فإن إفادة العلم خاصة ثالثة للنظر كما اعترف هو به فجاز أن يقتصر على إحدى الخواص لأن ذكر الكل غير واجب، وفساده ظاهر لخروج ما يطلب به الظن مطلقاً ولأن هذه الخاصة التي اكتفى بها مع ذكر العلم ( غير شاملة لأفراده فلا يكون جامعاً ) إذ قد يخرج ما يطلب به الظن الخالي عن الغلبة المفسرة بما ذكره، وأما الاكتفاء بأحدى الخاصتين أو الخواص، فإنما يصح في الخواص الشاملة. قوله : (إذ يوجب جوابه إلخ النظر الأول نقض إجمالي لدليل صحة الاكتفاء بغلبة الظن والنظر الثاني حل له بأن الاكتفاء بإحدى الخواص إنما هو في الخواص الشاملة، ونحن فيه ليس هذا القبيل، وقد يقال : إن كل واحد منها خاصة شاملة للنظر، فيجوز الاكتفاء بكل واحد منها، وذكر الاثنين والثلاثة لأن المراد يقولنا : الذي يطلب به العلم أن شأنه هذا، ولذا أورد صيغة المضارع لا أنه يطلب به العلم بالفعل، ولما كان الفكر في صورتي العلم والظن متحداً لأنه حركة في المعاني طلباً للمبادي، يصدق على كل نظر أنه حركة في المعاني من شأنه أن يطلب به علم، وظن وغلبة ظن فتدبر، ولا تلتفت إلى الشكوك التي نشأت فلتة من
And al-Āmidī answered it by saying that it (i.e., nazar) has two properties: providing the origin of conjecture and providing its predominance by its strength and power increasing, approaching certainty. And he sufficed in his definition with mentioning one of them, i.e., one of the two properties, and mentioning all, i.e., all its properties, is not obligatory. But there is a critique: this answer necessitates allowing satisfaction with saying 'that by which knowledge is sought', for providing knowledge is a third property of nazar, as he himself admitted, so it would be permissible to restrict to one of the properties because mentioning all is not obligatory. Yet its corruption is apparent because it excludes that by which conjecture is sought absolutely, and because this property with which he sufficed, along with mentioning knowledge, is not comprehensive for its individuals, so it would not be comprehensive. For it might exclude that by which conjecture devoid of predominance, as interpreted by what he mentioned, is sought. As for sufficing with one of the two properties or properties, it is only valid for comprehensive properties. He said: (Since his answer necessitates, etc.) The first consideration is a general refutation of the proof of the validity of sufficing with predominance of conjecture, and the second consideration is a solution to it, that sufficing with one of the properties is only for comprehensive properties, and our case is not of this kind. And it may be said that each one of them is a comprehensive property for nazar, so sufficing with each one is permissible, and mentioning two or three because what is meant by our saying 'that by which knowledge is sought' is that its character is this; hence the present tense was used, not that it actually seeks knowledge. Since thought in the two forms of knowledge and conjecture is unified because it is a movement among meanings seeking premises, it applies to every nazar that it is a movement among meanings whose character is to seek knowledge, conjecture, and predominance of conjecture. So reflect, and do not pay attention to the doubts that arose accidentally from...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer regarding the definition of nazar. Al-Āmidī argued that nazar has two properties: providing the origin of conjecture (aṣl al-ẓann) and providing its predominance (ghālibat al-ẓann). Since the definition only mentions one of these (seeking knowledge), it suffices because mentioning all properties is not required. The commentator critiques this: if mentioning one property is sufficient, then why not use 'that by which knowledge is sought' alone, since providing knowledge is a third property? This would exclude cases where conjecture is sought, thus the definition would not be comprehensive. Moreover, the property of seeking knowledge is not all-inclusive; it does not cover all individuals of nazar. The commentator further explains that sufficing with one property is only acceptable when that property is comprehensive (shāmila) for all individuals. Since neither property alone is fully comprehensive, Āmidī's defense fails. A possible counter-response is that each property is indeed comprehensive: whatever is a movement among meanings for premises can be described as seeking knowledge, conjecture, or predominance of conjecture. However, the commentator implies this reasoning is flawed because, for example, a single report (khabar al-wāḥid) cannot be said to be such that knowledge is sought by it, and a certain book cannot be said to be such that conjecture is sought by it.

Key Terms
النظر
الخاصية
أصل الظن
غلبة الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Āmidī, how many properties does nazar have?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Āmidī argues that nazar has two properties and that mentioning one is sufficient; his argument hinges on the principle that not all properties need be mentioned in a definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Āmidī argues that a definition need not list all properties of a thing. In your own understanding of tawḥīd, do you sometimes get caught up in detailed attributes while missing the single comprehensive reality? What would it mean for you to define your faith with one overarching truth?
?
The definition of nazar centers on seeking knowledge. When was the last time you sincerely sought knowledge in Islamic matters with the aim of reaching near-certainty, not just satisfying curiosity? Are you willing to put in the effort to move from conjecture to near-certainty in your beliefs?

Critique of Āmidī's Two-Property Argument
The commentator critiques Āmidī's argument and discusses comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique that Āmidī's argument is flawed because it fails to ensure comprehensiveness of the definition.

Core Idea
A definition must be comprehensive; a property that does not apply to all individuals cannot define the whole.

The commentator now criticizes al-Āmidī's response. He argues that if mentioning one property suffices, then why not define nazar simply as 'that by which knowledge is sought'? That would exclude cases where conjecture is sought. Moreover, the property of seeking knowledge is not comprehensive for all individuals of nazar. This lesson walks through the two main criticisms and a possible counter-response.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the Arabic text and translation. The commentator begins with 'But there is a critique' (وفيه نظر).
2
Understand the first criticism
The commentator argues that Āmidī's reasoning would allow defining nazar solely by seeking knowledge, but that excludes cases seeking conjecture, which is clearly part of nazar.
3
Understand the second criticism
The property of seeking knowledge is not comprehensive for all individuals of nazar, so it cannot serve as a universal definition.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer regarding the definition of nazar. Misconception: That any one property of nazar can define it even if it does not apply to all individuals.

Objection and Reply: The Definition of Nazar as More Than a Division
Refutation of the objection that the definition is mere division counting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the definition of nazar against the objection that it is merely a counting of divisions, and the distinction between a real definition and a formal definition (rasm).

Core Idea
A definition can be formal, identifying a concept by a property that covers all its individuals.

This lesson takes us through a critical objection to the definition of nazar: that it only lists its divisions and does not truly define it. The commentator's reply reveals a key subtlety in Islamic logic—the difference between a simple counting (taʿdīd) and a formal definition (taʿrīf rasmī) that uses a peculiar property (khāṣṣa) to capture the essence in a way that applies to all instances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: it claims the definition is only a counting of divisions, not a true definition. Identify the minor and major premises.
The objection uses a syllogistic form.
2
Examine the reply's strategy
Notice how the commentator first rejects the premise (we do not accept that it is a counting), then explains that the definition uses a property (the division into two) but adjusts it to apply to all individuals by taking the common denominator.
This adjustment is crucial.
3
Grasp the concept of a formal definition
Understand that the definition is a formal definition (rasm), which differs from a real definition (ḥadd). A formal definition identifies something by a peculiarity that distinguishes it, not by its quiddity.
This is a key logical point in Islamic thought.
4
Reflect on the commentator's closing remark
The commentator says this subtlety is hidden from most people. Reflect on why this distinction matters for defining terms in theology and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث التحديد إنما من قوله : (التحديد إلخ) تقرير السؤال أن ما ذكره القاضي تعديد لأقسام النظر، ولا شيء من التعديد بتحديد أما الصغرى فلان ما يطلب به العلم وما يطلب به الظن قسمان داخلان تحت النظر، وأما الكبرى فلان التعديد بيان للاقسام والتحديد بيان لمفهوم الشيء من حيث هو، وحاصل الجواب أنا لا نسلم أنه تعديد الأقسامه بل هو شرح لمفهومه باعتبار خاصته أعني الانقسام إليهما، إلا أنه لما كانت خاصة لمفهومه فقط غير صادقة على أفراده أخذ تلك الخاصة بحيث تكون صادقة على أفراده بأن أخذ القدر المشترك بين القسمين ورد فيما هو سبب لانقسامه إليهما، فقيل : الفكر الذي يطلب به أحد الأمرين أيهما كان فهو تعريف رسمي فتدبر، فإنه مما خفي على الأقوام وزل فيه الأقدام.
The third question: The definition is only from his saying (The definition, etc.): The formulation of the question is that what the judge mentioned is a counting of the divisions of nazar, and no counting is a definition. The minor premise is that 'that by which knowledge is sought' and 'that by which conjecture is sought' are two divisions falling under nazar. The major premise is that counting is an exposition of divisions, while definition is an exposition of the concept of a thing as such. The summary of the answer is: We do not accept that it is a counting of its divisions; rather it is an explanation of its concept in terms of its property, namely its division into the two. However, since the property of its concept alone is not true of its individuals, he took that property in such a way that it becomes true of its individuals by taking the common denominator between the two divisions and returning to what is the cause of its division into them. Thus it was said: 'The thought by which either of the two matters is sought, whichever it may be' is a formal definition (taʿrīf rasmī). So reflect, for it is something hidden from peoples and a slippery slope for feet.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection to the definition of nazar: that it is merely a counting (taʿdīd) of its divisions, not a true definition (taḥdīd). The objection argues that the definition lists 'that by which knowledge is sought' and 'that by which conjecture is sought' as two divisions under nazar, and listing divisions is not defining the essence. The reply states that the definition is not a simple counting; rather, it explains the concept of nazar by referencing its property (khāṣṣa), i.e., its division into these two categories. But since a property of the concept alone may not apply to all individuals, the author took the property in a way that does apply: he took the common denominator between the two divisions (i.e., the thought that seeks either knowledge or conjecture) and linked it to the cause of division (i.e., the different results). Thus, the definition becomes a formal definition (taʿrīf rasmī) that identifies nazar by a peculiarity that covers all its individuals. The commentator notes that this subtlety is often misunderstood.

Key Terms
التحديد
التعديد
الرسم
الخاصية
المعنى المشترك
القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a definition must always be a real definition (ḥadd) that states the essence (quiddity), and cannot be a formal definition (rasm) that identifies by a property.
Correction: A formal definition that uses a peculiar property (khāṣṣa) applying to all individuals is valid and sufficient for identification. The definition of nazar is of this type, not a simple counting of divisions.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator refute the claim that the definition of nazar is only a counting of divisions?
A) By arguing that counting divisions is a valid form of definition.
B) By showing the definition actually explains the concept through a property that covers all individuals.
C) By noting that the definition uses the word 'thought' not 'nazar'.
D) By claiming that the two divisions are not exhaustive.

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar is a formal definition (rasm) that identifies it through a property (its division into knowledge-seeking and conjecture-seeking) refined to cover all individuals, not a mere counting of divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own pursuit of understanding Islamic theology, have you ever dismissed a definition as mere academic jargon? How might such dismissal have hindered your grasp of a critical concept? Commit to re-examining one definition you've overlooked and write it down.
?
The text warns that this nuance is hidden from many and causes people to slip. Think of a time you misunderstood a religious concept because you only had a surface-level understanding. How could adopting the precision shown here change your approach to studying creed?
?
When you engage in nazar (speculative thought) about matters of faith, do you define it merely by its outcome (knowledge or conjecture) or do you seek its essential property? How might this affect the sincerity and depth of your intellectual journey?

The Third Objection: 'Aw' in the Definition of Nazar
Objection and reply regarding the word 'aw' in the definition of nazar, distinguishing between hesitation and division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the use of the word 'aw' in the definition of nazar does not introduce ambiguity and how it functions to divide the defined term into its two types.

Core Idea
The word 'aw' in definitions can indicate division, not hesitation, preserving clarity.

This lesson tackles a critical objection: how can a definition use the word 'or' without causing uncertainty? The author defends the definition by clarifying that 'aw' here serves to divide the defined term into its two distinct parts, not to introduce doubt. Through this discussion, you will see how careful logical analysis resolves apparent contradictions in theological definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: objection, answer, rephrased objection, answer.
The passage contains three formulations of the objection and two answers.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'aw'
The objector claims 'aw' is for hesitation (tardedid). The author says it is for division (taqsim). Understand this distinction.
Tardid implies ambiguity; taqsim implies classification.
3
Examine the third formulation
The third objection states that both division and hesitation contradict definition. The reply explains that the definition provides two definitions for two parts.
This clarifies that the definition is not a single ambiguous limit but two separate limits for two realities.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this shows the importance of precise language in kalam definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد يقرر هذا السؤال الثالث في هذا الموضع وغيره من الحدود المشتملة على الترديد بعبارة أخرى فيقال : لفظة أو للترديد وهو للإبهام فينافي التحديد الذي يقصد به البيان. والجواب منع كونه للترديد بل هو للتقسيم، أي أياً كان من القسمين المذكورين في هذا الحد فهو من المحدود. وحاصله أن المراد بـ "أو" تقسيم المحدود لا تقسيم الحد.

ثم قال : ( أو للترديد لأنه موضوع لأحد الأمرين من غير تعيين. )

وحاصل هذا السؤال: أن لفظة أو للترديد، والترديد للإبهام، فينافي التحديد. والجواب: منع كونه للترديد بل هو للتقسيم.

وقد يقرر السؤال الثالث أيضاً بأن يقال: كلمة أو للتقسيم أو للترديد، وكلاهما ينافي التحديد؛ لأن التقسيم يقتضي أن للمحدود أقساماً، والتعريف لا يكون إلا بالماهية المشتركة. وأجيب بأن المقصود من هذا التعريف هو إعطاء حدين لقسمي النظر المختلفين، لا تعريف واحد منهما. فهو في الحقيقة حدان لقسميه المتخالفين في الحقيقة المخصوصة المتشاركين في ماهية مطلق النظر، ولم يرد بـ "أو" الحد إما هذا وإما ذاك على سبيل الشك أو التشكيك لينافي التحديد.
He said: 'And this third question may be posed in this place and other definitions containing disjunction in another expression, such that it is said: The word "aw" is for disjunction, and disjunction is for ambiguity, which contradicts definition whose purpose is clarification.' The answer is to deny that it is for disjunction; rather it is for division, i.e., whichever of the two divisions mentioned in this definition it may be, it is part of the defined term. The sum total is that what is intended by "aw" is division of the defined term, not division of the definition.

Then he said: 'Or it is for disjunction because it is placed for one of two things without specification.'

The sum of this question: The word "aw" is for disjunction, and disjunction is for ambiguity, so it contradicts definition. The answer: Denial that it is for disjunction; rather it is for division.

And this third question may also be posed by saying: The word "aw" is for division or disjunction, and both contradict definition; because division requires that the defined term has parts, and definition is only of the common essence. And the answer is that the intent of this definition is to give two definitions for the two different parts of nazar, not one definition for either. So in reality it is two definitions for its two parts that are different in specific reality and share in the essence of absolute nazar, and He did not intend by "aw" the limit either this or that on the way of doubt or skepticism to contradict definition.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection to the definition of nazar. The objector argues that the word 'aw' (or) in the definition introduces either hesitation (tardedid) or division (taqsim), both of which are incompatible with a proper definition. The author responds by denying that 'aw' is used for hesitation; rather it is used for division of the defined term itself. The commentator clarifies that the definition actually provides two separate definitions for the two distinct parts of nazar (the part that yields knowledge and the part that yields strong opinion). Thus, the word 'aw' indicates that the limit applies to either of the two types, not that there is doubt. The passage then rephrases the objection and answer, emphasizing that the aim is to give two definitions for two different realities sharing the common essence of absolute thought.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any use of 'aw' in a definition always introduces doubt and invalidates the definition.
Correction: The word 'aw' can be used for division of the defined term into its types, which is valid and does not introduce ambiguity, as shown in this passage.

The word 'aw' in the definition of nazar is used for division of the defined term into its two types, not for hesitation, thus preserving the definition's clarity.

The Fourth Objection: Is 'Fikr' Redundant in the Definition of Nazar?
Objection and reply regarding the necessity of 'fikr' in the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'fikr' is not redundant but serves as the genus of nazar, making it essential for a complete definition.

Core Idea
Fikr is the genus, nazar is the species — removing the genus makes the definition incomplete.

The fourth objection claims that the word 'fikr' is unnecessary because the rest of the definition already says 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought.' But this misses a key logical point: nazar is a type of thought, and without naming the genus, the definition is incomplete. Let's see how the reply clarifies this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the objection carefully: it asserts that 'fikr' is redundant. Note its reasoning.
The objector thinks the phrase 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought' alone is sufficient.
2
Study the Reply
Read the reply which explains that 'fikr' here means mental imaginary movements and serves as the genus of nazar.
The reply distinguishes between mental and physical movements.
3
Understand Genus and Species
Grasp the logical relationship: 'fikr' is the broader category (genus), and 'nazar' is a specific type (species) of fikr directed at knowledge or opinion.
Without the genus, the definition would be incomplete.
4
Reflect on Imam al-Haramayn's Statement
Consider the quote that fikr may or may not be for seeking knowledge; only when it does is it called nazar.
This reinforces the genus-species distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: لفظ الفكر في هذا الحد زائد، لا حاجة إليه، إذ باقي الحد مغن عنه، فإنه يكفي أن يقال: النظر هو الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن.

والجواب: أن المراد بالفكر هاهنا هو الحركات التخيلية الذهنية لا العينية المحسوسة، فلا يكون منافياً لما قيل: من أن حركة الذهن إذا كانت في المعقولات تسمى فكراً، وإذا كانت في المحسوسات تسمى تَخيُّلاً. وكيف كانت، أي سواء طلب بها علم أو ظن أو لم يطلب. قال إمام الحرمين: الفكر قد يكون لطلب علم أو ظن فيسمى نظراً، وقد لا يكون فلا يسمى. فالفكر جنس للنظر، والباقي فصل.
The fourth question: The word 'fakr' in this definition is redundant, no need for it, since the rest of the definition suffices instead of it, for it is sufficient to say: al-nazar is that by which knowledge or opinion is sought.

The answer: That what is meant by 'fakr' here is the imaginary mental movements, not physical sensible ones, so it does not contradict what was said: That the movement of the mind when it is in intelligibles is called 'fakr', and when in sensibles is called 'takhayyul'. And however it may be, i.e., whether knowledge or opinion is sought by it or not. Imam al-Haramayn said: al-fakr may be for seeking knowledge or opinion and then is called nazar, and may not be and then is not called nazar. So al-fakr is the genus of nazar, and the rest is the differentia.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection, which claims that the word 'fakr' (thought) in the definition of nazar is redundant because the phrase 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought' would suffice without it. The answer explains that 'fakr' is essential because it serves as the genus (jins) of nazar. Nazar is defined as a particular type of thought, specifically thought directed towards acquiring knowledge or opinion. Without mentioning 'fakr', the definition would lack the genus. Moreover, 'fakr' here does not refer to any random thought but to imaginary mental movements (al-harakat al-takhayyuliyya al-dhihniyya), as opposed to physical movements. Imam al-Haramayn is quoted to support that thought may or may not be directed towards knowledge, and only when it is so directed is it called nazar. Thus, 'fakr' is the broader category, and the rest of the definition specifies the differentia.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removing 'fakr' from the definition would not change its meaning, as the rest already specifies the purpose.
Correction: But 'fakr' is the genus of nazar; without it, the definition would lack the broader category of thought, making it incomplete.

Checkpoint

D) Because the definition requires three words.

Key Takeaway
The term 'fikr' is essential in the definition of nazar because it provides the genus; nazar is a species of thought directed at knowledge or opinion.

The Definition of Nazar and the Role of Fikr
Sanusi defines nazar as fikr and answers the objection that 'fikr' is redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines nazar and why the word 'fikr' is not redundant in the definition.

Core Idea
Fikr specifies the type of mental movement that constitutes nazar.

Sanusi begins by defining nazar in two ways, then answers an objection about the word 'fikr'. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution, clarifying the precise meaning of 'fikr' in the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two definitions of nazar and the objection that follows.
The passage is from the Fifth Observatory, First Objective.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that 'fikr' is redundant. Understand why: the rest of the definition seems enough without it.
3
Understand the answer
Sanusi answers that 'fikr' here means imaginative mental movements, not sensory ones. This distinguishes nazar from other mental activities.
Imam al-Haramayn's saying reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : في تعريفه. المحدود حده هذا، وهو أنه الفكر الذي يطلب به علم، وقسماً آخر أنه الفكر الذي يطلب به ظن، فهو في الحقيقة حدان لقسميه المتخالفين في الحقيقة المخصوصة المتشاركين في ماهية مطلق النظر، ولم يرد بأحد الحدين إما هذا وإما ذاك على سبيل الشك أو التشكيك لينافي التحديد. السؤال: لفظ (الفكر) في هذا الحد زائد لا حاجة إليه، إذ باقي الحد مغن عنه، فإنه يكفي أن يقال: النظر هو الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن. والجواب أن المراد بالفكر هاهنا هو الحركات التخيلية أي الذهنية لا العينية المحسوسة، فلا يكون منافياً لما قيل: من أن حركة الذهن إذا كانت في المعقولات تسمى فكراً، وإذا كانت في المحسوسات تسمى تخيلاً. كيف كانت، أي سواء طلب بها علم أو ظن أو لم يطلب. قال إمام الحرمين: الفكر قد يكون لطلب علم أو ظن فيسمى نظراً، وقد لا يكون فلا يسمى.
The Fifth Observatory - First Objective: On its Definition. The defined is this: it is the thought by which knowledge is sought, and another division: it is the thought by which opinion is sought. So in reality they are two definitions for its two divisions that differ in their specific reality but share in the quiddity of absolute nazar. He did not intend by either of the two definitions this one or that one in a doubtful or equivocal manner that would contradict definition. The question: The word 'al-fikr' in this definition is redundant and unnecessary, since the rest of the definition suffices without it, for it is enough to say: 'al-nazar is that by which knowledge or opinion is sought.' The answer: That what is meant by 'al-fikr' here is the imaginative movements, i.e., mental ones, not sensory physical ones, so it does not contradict what has been said: that the movement of the mind when in conceptual matters is called 'fikr', and when in sensory matters is called 'takhayyul'. In whatever manner, i.e., whether by it knowledge or opinion is sought or not. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'al-fikr may be for seeking knowledge or opinion and is then called nazar, and it may not be, and then it is not called nazar.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines nazar and answers an objection. Sanusi states that nazar is defined in two ways: as thought seeking knowledge and as thought seeking opinion. The objector says that the word 'fikr' is redundant because the definition could simply say 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought'. The answer explains that 'fikr' is specified to mean mental imaginative movements, not sensory movements, so it excludes movements that are not called fikr. The point is that not all mental movements are nazar; only those seeking knowledge or opinion are nazar. Thus 'fikr' is necessary to indicate the type of movement.

Misconception: One might think that the word 'fikr' is redundant in the definition of nazar.
Correction: The author explains that 'fikr' specifies the type of movement (imaginative mental movements), which excludes sensory movements, making it necessary.

B) Mental imaginative movements
C) Sensory movements

Key Takeaway
Nazar is a specific kind of fikr that seeks knowledge or opinion; fikr is needed to indicate that nazar involves mental imaginative movements, not any movement.

Definition of Nazar: The Role of Genus and Differentia
The author defines nazar as thought seeking knowledge or opinion, and defends the inclusion of both genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) are necessary in the definition of nazar, and how this logical structure prevents misunderstandings about what nazar includes.

Core Idea
A definition needs both genus and differentia: genus reveals the essence, differentia distinguishes.

In defining nazar, the author of the Mawāqif carefully constructs a definition using the logical tools of genus and differentia. He anticipates an objection: why not just use the differentia? His response shows that the genus is essential for pointing to the essence of the thing. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance for understanding the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: definition, objection, response.
2
Identify the definition's components
Find the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) in the definition. The genus is 'fkr' (thought) and the differentia is 'that by which knowledge or opinion is sought'.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the differentia alone suffices for distinction. Why might someone think that? Consider what a differentia does.
The objection is presented as 'لا يقال' — it is a hypothetical counterargument.
4
Analyze the response
The author argues that the genus indicates the essence of the thing, while the differentia completes and distinguishes. Without the genus, the definition might incorrectly include other things that also 'seek' knowledge.
The response uses a rhetorical question: 'Do you not see that...?'
5
Reflect on the preventive role of genus
Consider how the genus prevents over-inclusion. The author mentions that without the genus, the definition might be understood to include life or intellect, which also have a role in seeking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به كأكثر حديث النفس (فهو) بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه جنس للنظر لا مرادف له على ما هو المتعارف، والباقي من الحد فصل له يميزه عن سائر الحركات التخيلية. ولا يقال: أن الفصل كاف في التمييز والجنس مستغن عنه في الحد، كيف والجنس هو الذي يدل على أصل الماهية والفصل يحصلها ويميزها؟ ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت: النظر هو الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن، لم يفهم منه أن أصل ماهية النظر بل ربما أوهم شموله لغير النظر مما له مدخل في ذلك الطلب.
It is, like most inner speech, (so it) in the sense we mentioned, a genus for nazar, not a synonym in the usual way, and the remainder of the definition is a differentia that distinguishes it from other imaginative movements. And it is not said: that the differentia is sufficient for distinction and the genus is unnecessary in the definition. How? For the genus is that which indicates the essence of the thing, and the differentia actualizes it and distinguishes it. Do you not see that if you say: 'nazar is that by which knowledge or opinion is sought,' it is not understood from that the essence of nazar, but rather it might give the impression it includes other things that have a role in that seeking.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a definition with objection and response. The author defines nazar as "الفكر الذي يطلب به العلم أو الظن" (thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought). The genus of this definition is "الفكر" (thought), and the remaining part (الباقي) is the differentia that distinguishes it from other imaginative movements. The objection: one might say that the differentia alone suffices for distinction, and the genus is unnecessary. The response: the genus points to the essence of the thing, while the differentia completes and distinguishes it. Without the genus, the definition could give the impression that it includes other things that have a role in seeking, such as life or intellect, etc. So both are necessary.

فكر
جنس
فصل

ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The differentia alone is enough for a definition; the genus is extraneous.
Correction: The genus is essential because it points to the essence of the thing, without which the definition may incorrectly include other entities that share the differentia.

Checkpoint

B) Because it points to the essence of the thing
C) Because it is the only part that distinguishes
D) Because the fasl is not necessary

Key Takeaway
The genus is essential in a definition because it indicates the essence, while the differentia distinguishes; both are necessary for a complete definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in nazar (reflective thought) about your faith, do you ensure you have a clear definition of what you are seeking? How might lacking both genus and differentia in your thinking lead to confusion?
?
The author emphasizes that a definition must capture both the essence and the distinction. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, have you ever relied on a partial definition that missed the essence? How does this lesson challenge you to seek deeper, more precise knowledge?
?
Reflect on your daily thoughts: are they simply 'inner speech' without purpose (genus only) or are they directed toward a specific goal (differentia)? How can you combine both to make your nazar more effective in worship?

The Second Definition of Nazar and Its Objection
A second definition of nazar and its insufficiency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of nazar proposed by the masters of instruction and the key objection that it fails to encompass all types of nazar, particularly definitions by fasl alone or khassa alone.

Core Idea
A definition of nazar must include all its forms, even incomplete ones.

The second definition of nazar comes from the school that sees nazar as acquiring the unknown from known premises. They define it as 'the ordering of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter.' However, this definition is criticized for being incomplete. In this lesson, we examine the definition and the first objection raised against it, including a colorful remark from Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by situating the definition within a particular school.
2
Identify the definition
Locate the definition of nazar given by the 'masters of instruction'. What are its key components?
The definition is: 'ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى أمر آخر'.
3
Understand the objection
The objection is that the definition is not comprehensive (غير جامع). Why? Because it excludes definitions by fasl alone and khassa alone, which are valid types of nazar.
These excluded types are considered deficient (نزر خداج).
4
Examine Ibn Sina's critique
Ibn Sina's statement 'لا يشفي غليلاً' is cited to emphasize the deficiency. Understand the linguistic nuance of 'ghaleel' (thirst/illness).
Both meanings underscore incompleteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله تعريفات بحسب المذاهب فمن يرى أنه (أي النظر) اكتساب المجهول بالمعلومات السابقة على ذلك المجهول وهم أرباب التعاليم القائلون بالتعليم والتعلم للمجهولات من المعلومات قالوا: (النظر) ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى أمر آخر وعليه إشكالان أحدهما: أنه غير جامع لخروج التعريف بالفصل والخاصة وحدهما أي تعريف المجهول التصوري بالفصل وحده وبالخاصة وحدها فإن هذا التعريف من أقسام النظر مع خروجه عن حده وكونه أي كون التعريف بالفصل وحده أو بالخاصة وحدها نزراً قليلاً خداجاً ناقصاً كما قال ابن سينا: لا يشفي غليلاً لأن هذا الحد إنما هو لمطلق النظر، فيجب أن يندرج فيه جميع أفراده التامة والناقصة. قوله: (لا يشفي غليلاً) بالشين المعجمة والفاء والغليل بالغين المعجمة العطش وشدته وحرارة الجوف وقد جاء صفة مشبهة يقال: غل فهو غليل كما في القاموس وكلا المعنيين يصح هاهنا، ويجوز أن يكون بالعين المهملة صفة مشبهة من العلة بمعنى المرض.
And it has definitions according to the schools. For the one who sees that it (i.e., nazar) is the acquisition of the unknown from preceding known premises regarding that unknown, and they are the masters of instruction who hold teaching and learning of unknowns from knowns, they said: (nazar) is the ordering of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter. There are two objections to it: first, that it is not comprehensive because it excludes definition by differentia alone and by special property alone, i.e., the definition of the conceptual unknown by differentia alone and by special property alone, for this definition is one of the divisions of nazar while it is outside its scope, and that (the definition by differentia alone or special property alone) is a little deficient, incomplete, as Ibn Sina said: it does not satisfy thirst, because this definition is for absolute nazar, so it must include all its complete and incomplete individuals. His statement: (it does not satisfy thirst) with sheen and fa, and ghaleel with ghain is thirst and its intensity and heat of the stomach, and it has come as a quasi-adjective; it is said: ghall, so he is ghaleel, as in al-Qamus. Both meanings are valid here, and it is possible that it is with the unpointed 'ayn as a quasi-adjective from 'illa in the sense of illness.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents a second definition of nazar from the school that views nazar as acquiring the unknown from known premises. They define nazar as "ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى أمر آخر" (ordering known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter). Two objections are raised: First, it is not comprehensive because it excludes definitions by fasl alone and by khassa alone (i.e., defining a conceptual unknown by only the differentia or only the special property). Such definitions are valid types of nazar but are not included in this definition. Second, those definitions are deficient (نزر خداج) and, as Ibn Sina said, "لا يشفي غليلاً" (does not satisfy thirst). But the definition is supposed to cover all forms of nazar, including incomplete ones. The commentary then explains the phrase "لا يشفي غليلاً" linguistically, noting two possible readings: with shin and fa meaning thirst, or with 'ayn meaning illness.

تأدي
تعليم
تعلم
نزر
خداج
غليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second definition of nazar is complete and covers all types.
Correction: It fails to include definitions by fasl alone and khassa alone, which are valid but deficient forms of nazar.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection to the definition 'ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى أمر آخر'?
A) It is too broad
B) It excludes definitions by fasl alone and khassa alone
C) It does not produce certainty
D) It is not a definition

Key Takeaway
The second definition of nazar is rejected because it does not cover all valid types, particularly definitions using only the differentia or special property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own quest for knowledge, have you ever defined a concept in a way that excluded obvious examples, just to keep it neat? How did that limit your understanding?
?
Ibn Sina says a deficient definition 'does not satisfy thirst'. When has your own incomplete understanding left you spiritually or intellectually dissatisfied? What did you do about it?
?
The author insists that a definition of nazar must include even its deficient forms. Are there areas in your life—faith, worship, relationships—where you have excluded genuine but imperfect expressions? Will you now broaden your definition to include them?

Al-Qadi's Definition of Nazar and Its Comprehensive Divisions
Commentator explains al-Qadi's definition of nazar and its inclusive divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's definition of nazar as 'thought seeking knowledge or opinion' and why it is considered comprehensive, covering all types including valid/invalid, certain/conjectural, and those leading to conception or assent.

Core Idea
Nazar is thought seeking knowledge or opinion — and it includes every form, even the deficient ones.

Al-Qadi's definition of nazar is a cornerstone in Islamic logic. The commentator unpacks this definition, showing how it encompasses all divisions of speculation. We'll see why this definition is preferred over alternatives, and we'll explore the objections to a narrower definition proposed by some philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source text once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where al-Qadi defines nazar. The key phrase is 'thought seeking knowledge or opinion'.
This appears at the beginning of the commentary.
3
List the divisions mentioned
The passage mentions divisions: valid/fasid, qat'i/zanni, and those leading to tasawwur or tasdiq. Make a simple list.
These pairs show the comprehensive nature.
4
Understand the objection
Read the part about the alternative definition (arbāb al-taʿālīm) and the two objections: it is not comprehensive and it excludes some types of nazar.
The objection is that the definition should include all individuals, even deficient ones.
5
Reflect on comprehensiveness
Consider why including invalid and deficient forms is important for a definition of absolute nazar.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا الحد الذي ذكره القاضي (تعريفه الشامل لجميع أقسامه الصحيح والفاسد والقطعي، والظني والموصل إلى التصور سواء كان في مفرد أو مركب والموصل إلى التصديق على اختلاف أقسامه (وله) أي للنظر ( تعريفات بحسب المذاهب فمن يرى أنه ( أي النظر ( اكتساب المجهول بالمعلومات السابقة ) على ذلك المجهول (وهم أرباب التعاليم القائلون بالتعليم والتعلم للمجهولات من المعلومات ( قالوا : ( النظر ( ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى ) أمر (آخر وعليه إشكالان أحدهما : أنه غير جامع لخروج التعريف بالفصل والخاصة وحدهما ) أي تعريف المجهول التصوري بالفصل وحده وبالخاصة وحدها فإن هذا التعريف من أقسام النظر مع خروجه عن حده ) وكونه) أي كون التعريف بالفصل وحده أو بالخاصة وحدها ( نزراً ) قليلاً ( خداجاً ) ناقصاً ) كما قال ابن سينا : لا يشفي غليلاً) لأن هذا الحد إنما هو لمطلق النظر ، فيجب أن يندرج فيه جميع أفراده التامة والناقصة
قوله : ( الشامل لجميع أقسامه لأن جميع أفراده يطلب به العلم أو الظن سواء حصل أو لا . قوله : ( والقطعي) باعتبار مادته وصورته كالنظر القياسي البرهاني. قوله: (والظني) من حيث المادة كالنظر القياسي الخطابي، أو من حيث الصورة: كالاستقراء والتمثيل. قوله : على اختلاف أقسامه من اليقيني والظني والجهلي فإن النظر الواقع لتحصيلها فكر يطلب به العلم أو الظن إذ العاقل لا يطلب الجهل المركب .
So this is the definition mentioned by al-Qadi (his definition encompassing all its divisions: valid and invalid, certain and conjectural, leading to conception whether in a single [term] or compound, and leading to assent according to its various divisions). And it (i.e., nazar) has definitions according to schools. So whoever holds that it (i.e., nazar) is acquisition of the unknown from preceding known things (concerning that unknown)—and they are the masters of instruction who affirm teaching and learning of unknowns from known things—they said: (nazar is) the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter. And there are two objections to it: one is that it is not comprehensive because it excludes definition by differentia alone and by property alone (i.e., the definition of the conceptual unknown by differentia alone and by property alone, for this definition is a type of nazar while being outside its definition), and that it (i.e., the definition by differentia alone or property alone) is scanty (little) and deficient (incomplete), as Ibn Sina said: it does not quench the thirst. Because this definition is for absolute nazar, so it must include all its individuals, complete and incomplete. He said: (the encompassing of all its divisions) because all its individuals seek by it knowledge or opinion, whether attained or not. He said: (and the certain) with respect to its matter and form, like demonstrative syllogistic reasoning. He said: (and the conjectural) with respect to matter, like rhetorical syllogistic reasoning, or with respect to form, like induction and analogy. He said: (according to its various divisions) of certain, conjectural, and ignorance-based; for the nazar that occurs to obtain them is thought seeking knowledge or opinion, since the rational person does not seek compounded ignorance.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (speculation) given by al-Qadi (al-Baydawi). The commentator first presents the definition: nazar is 'thought seeking knowledge or opinion' (fikr yutlab bihi al-'ilm aw al-zann). He notes that this definition is comprehensive, covering all divisions: valid and invalid, certain and conjectural, and it leads either to conception (tasawwur) whether simple or compound, or to assent (tasdiq) in all its types. The commentator then distinguishes other definitions by different schools. In particular, he mentions the definition by 'arbāb al-taʿālīm' (masters of instruction), who define nazar as 'the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to arrive at another matter.' He lists two objections to this definition. The first objection is that it is not comprehensive because it excludes definition by differentia alone (fasl) or by property alone (khāṣṣa). These are types of nazar but fall outside the stated definition. The commentator adds that such definitions are considered scanty (nazar) and deficient (khudāj), quoting Ibn Sina: 'it does not quench the thirst.' The essence of the objection is that the definition is for absolute nazar (muṭlaq al-naẓar), so it must include all its types, both complete and deficient. The commentator then explains the phrases from the base text: 'encompassing all its divisions' means that all forms of nazar seek knowledge or opinion whether they attain it or not. He further defines 'certain' nazar as that which is certain in matter and form, like demonstrative syllogisms; 'conjectural' nazar as conjectural in matter like rhetorical arguments, or in form like induction and analogy; and 'according to its various divisions' refers to certain, conjectural, and ignorance-based, for the nazar performed to obtain them is thought seeking knowledge or opinion, since a rational person does not seek compounded ignorance.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Qadi's definition includes invalid, conjectural, and even deficient forms, as long as it is thought aimed at knowledge or opinion.

Checkpoint

B) To seek knowledge or opinion
C) To arrange known matters
D) To avoid error

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's definition is comprehensive because it includes all forms of nazar, whether they lead to knowledge, opinion, or even error, as long as they are aimed at seeking knowledge or opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in thinking about matters of faith (like the existence of God or attributes), do you often settle for 'deficient' reasoning, such as a single incomplete definition, instead of striving for comprehensive understanding? What step will you take this week to pursue a more thorough nazar?
?
The source states that a rational person does not seek compounded ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). Have you ever convinced yourself you understand something about your religion when in fact your reasoning was flawed? How can you honestly confront that ignorance today?
?
If nazar is thought that seeks knowledge or opinion, what is the condition of your heart when you engage in such thought? Is it sincere seeking of truth, or are you more concerned with winning arguments? How can you purify your intention in your next act of reflection?

The Definition of Nazar by Arbāb al-Taʿālīm and the First Objection
Presents an alternative definition of nazar and the first objection of non-comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative definition of nazar as 'the arrangement of known or conjectured matters' and its first objection that it is not comprehensive because it excludes definition by differentia or property alone.

Core Idea
A definition must include all its instances, even the deficient ones.

This lesson introduces a different definition of nazar proposed by the 'masters of instruction' (arbāb al-taʿālīm). They define nazar as 'the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter.' However, this definition faces an objection: it fails to cover all types of nazar, such as definition by differentia alone. The commentator also quotes Ibn Sina's remark that such deficient definitions 'do not quench the thirst.' We will explore the definition, the objection, and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a definition followed by an objection and a supporting quote.
2
Identify the alternative definition
Locate the definition of nazar given by arbāb al-taʿālīm: 'the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter.' Understand its components.
This definition emphasizes order and progression from known to unknown.
3
Understand the first objection
The objection is that the definition is not comprehensive (ghayr jāmiʿ). It excludes valid forms of nazar like definition by differentia alone. Ask yourself why this is a problem.
The definition must cover all cases of nazar, both complete and incomplete.
4
Reflect on Ibn Sina's critique
Ibn Sina's phrase 'lā yashfī ghalīlan' means 'it does not quench the thirst.' The commentator explains both possible readings of 'ghalīl.' This underscores the inadequacy of the definition.
Deficient definitions leave the seeker unsatisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله) أي للنظر ( تعريفات بحسب المذاهب فمن يرى أنه ( أي النظر ( اكتساب المجهول بالمعلومات السابقة ) على ذلك المجهول (وهم أرباب التعاليم القائلون بالتعليم والتعلم للمجهولات من المعلومات ( قالوا : ( النظر ( ترتيب أمور معلومة أو مظنونة للتأدي إلى ) أمر (آخر وعليه إشكالان أحدهما : أنه غير جامع لخروج التعريف بالفصل والخاصة وحدهما ) أي تعريف المجهول التصوري بالفصل وحده وبالخاصة وحدها فإن هذا التعريف من أقسام النظر مع خروجه عن حده ) وكونه) أي كون التعريف بالفصل وحده أو بالخاصة وحدها ( نزراً ) قليلاً ( خداجاً ) ناقصاً ) كما قال ابن سينا : لا يشفي غليلاً) لأن هذا الحد إنما هو لمطلق النظر ، فيجب أن يندرج فيه جميع أفراده التامة والناقصة
قوله : (لا يشفي غليلاً) بالشين المعجمة والفاء والغليل بالغين المعجمة العطش، وشدته وحرارة الجوف وقد جاء صفة مشبهة يقال : غل فهو غليل كما في القاموس وكلا المعنيين يصح هاهنا، ويجوز أن يكون بالعين المهملة صفة مشبهة من العلة بمعنى المرض.
And it (i.e., nazar) has definitions according to schools. So whoever holds that it (i.e., nazar) is acquisition of the unknown from preceding known things (concerning that unknown)—and they are the masters of instruction who affirm teaching and learning of unknowns from known things—they said: (nazar is) the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter. And there are two objections to it: one is that it is not comprehensive because it excludes definition by differentia alone and by property alone (i.e., the definition of the conceptual unknown by differentia alone and by property alone, for this definition is a type of nazar while being outside its definition), and that it (i.e., the definition by differentia alone or property alone) is scanty (little) and deficient (incomplete), as Ibn Sina said: it does not quench the thirst. Because this definition is for absolute nazar, so it must include all its individuals, complete and incomplete. He said: ('it does not quench the thirst') with the dotted shīn and fā', and 'al-ghalīl' with the dotted ghayn means thirst, its intensity, and heat of the interior, and it occurs as a descriptive adjective; one says: 'ghalla' so he is 'ghalīl' as in the Qamus. Both meanings are correct here, and it is possible that it is with the undotted 'ayn as a descriptive adjective from 'illa' meaning illness.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative definition of nazar by the 'masters of instruction' (arbāb al-taʿālīm), who define it as 'the arrangement of known or conjectured matters to lead to another matter.' The commentator notes two objections to this definition. The first objection is that it is not comprehensive (ghayr jāmiʿ) because it excludes a certain type of nazar: definition by differentia alone (fasl) or by property alone (khāṣṣa). For example, defining a conceptual unknown by its differentia or property alone is still a form of nazar, but it falls outside the given definition since it does not involve a clear arrangement of multiple known matters. The commentator adds that such definitions are considered 'scanty' (nazar) and 'deficient' (khudāj), quoting Ibn Sina's remark that 'it does not quench the thirst' (lā yashfī ghalīlan). The underlying reason is that the definition is meant for absolute nazar, so it must encompass all its instances, both complete and incomplete. The commentator then provides a linguistic note on the phrase 'lā yashfī ghalīlan', explaining that 'ghalīl' with the dotted ghayn means thirst or its intensity, and it can also be read with the undotted 'ayn to mean illness. This note serves to clarify the meaning of Ibn Sina's critique: the deficient definitions fail to satisfy the purpose of nazar.

Misconception: Some might think that the definition 'arrangement of known or conjectured matters' is complete and covers all types of nazar.
Correction: The objection shows that it excludes valid but deficient forms like definition by differentia alone, so it is not comprehensive.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A definition of nazar must be comprehensive enough to include all its forms, even those that are scanty or deficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn Sina criticizes a definition that 'does not quench the thirst.' In your own life, have you been satisfied with superficial knowledge of Allah's attributes or the proofs of His existence? How might you commit today to seeking a more comprehensive understanding?
?
The masters of instruction define nazar as arrangement of known matters. But the objection shows that definition must include even imperfect forms. When you share Islam with others, do you only present 'perfect' arguments, ignoring the value of simpler proofs? How can you use both complete and incomplete reasoning to help others?

The Refutation of Objections to Definition by Simple Meanings
Sanusi presents an answer and its refutation regarding definition using simple meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objections to definition by simple meanings and affirms its intellectual permissibility.

Core Idea
Definition by simple meanings is intellectually permissible despite the need for a mental indicator.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses an objection about defining things using simple (non-composite) meanings. He presents an answer that a mental indicator is required, then thoroughly refutes both parts of that answer, ultimately affirming the validity of such definitions. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the argument structure: objection, answer, refutation, conclusion.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the conclusion is clear from context.
2
Identify the answer's two parts
The answer claims that with differentia (fasl) and property (khassa) a mental indicator is needed, and that derived terms imply composition. Find these two points in the text.
They are introduced by 'لأنهما بحسب مفهوميهما أعم' and 'وأيضاً هما مشتقان'.
3
Study the refutations
Sanusi refutes both parts: the first because the indicator would invalidate the definition (unless one accepts incomplete definition), the second because definition by singular terms is not limited to derived terms.
The refutations are marked by 'أما الأول' and 'أما الثاني'.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The truth (الحق) is that definition by simple meanings is intellectually permissible (جائز عقلاً), resulting in a single movement from the defined to the principle.
This conclusion rebuts the earlier objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قل استعمالها أو كثر ، وقد أجيب أيضاً بأنه لا بد مع الفصل والخاصة من قرينة عقلية مخصصة، لأنهما بحسب مفهوميهما أعم من المحدود، فلا يتصور الانتقال منهما إليه إلا مع أمر زائد يكون بينهما ترتيب وأيضاً هما مشتقان ومعنى المشتق شيء له المشتق منه فهناك تركيب قطعاً، وكلاهما مردود أما الأول فلان اعتبار القرينة مع الفصل يخرجه عن كونه ،حداً، إلا أن يجوز الحد الناقص بالمركب من الداخل والخارج، وأما الثاني فلعدم انحصار التعريف بالمفرد في المشتقات والحق أن التعريف بالمعاني المفردة جائز عقلاً، فتكون هناك حركة واحدة من المطلوب إلى
It was said: whether its usage is rare or frequent, and it was also answered that with the differentia and property there must be a mental specifying indicator, because they are, according to their concepts, more general than the defined, so transition from them to it is not conceivable except with an additional matter that has arrangement between them, and also they are derived terms, and the meaning of a derived term is something that has the thing from which it is derived, so there is composition necessarily, and both are refuted: as for the first, because considering the indicator with the differentia removes it from being a definition, unless one allows the incomplete definition composed of internal and external; as for the second, because definition by singular terms is not confined to derived terms, and the truth is that definition by simple meanings is intellectually permissible, so there is one movement from the desired [to]
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument: an objection is implied about definitions using simple meanings, then an answer is given that a mental indicator is needed with the differentia (fasl) and property (khassa) because they are more general, and they are derived terms implying composition. Then both parts of the answer are refuted: first, adding an indicator removes it from being a proper definition (unless one accepts the incomplete definition); second, definition by singular terms is not limited to derived terms, and simple meanings are permissible, resulting in a single movement from the desired to the principle (mabda').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition by simple meanings is impossible due to composition.
Correction: Sanusi affirms that definition by simple meanings is intellectually permissible, despite the need for a mental indicator and the possibility of incomplete definition.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the passage regarding definition by simple meanings?
A) It is impossible because a mental indicator is required.
B) It is intellectually permissible.
C) It is only possible with derived terms.
D) It requires an incomplete definition.

Key Takeaway
Definition by simple meanings is intellectually permissible, as Sanusi refutes the objections by showing that the mental indicator does not invalidate it and that it is not confined to derived terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological concept, do you tend to accept it as a simple truth or do you mentally add conditions and indicators until it becomes complex? How might that affect the purity of your belief?
?
Sanusi argues that a simple meaning can be a valid definition. In your own understanding of Allah's names and attributes, do you sometimes feel the need to derive them or think of them as composite, rather than simply affirming them as they are? What might that reveal about your heart's reliance on your own reasoning?
?
Think of a time you assumed a definition required a mental 'crutch' to be valid. How did that assumption limit your understanding? Will you now allow yourself to accept clear, simple meanings without overcomplicating them?

The Simple Principle: How a Basic Concept Defines by Intentional Recall
Sanusi explains the simple principle's role in definition and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple principle can define without prior arrangement, the conditional nature of its entailment, and the role of the craft in definition.

Core Idea
A simple principle defines by being intentionally brought to mind, not by its mere existence.

In defining 'nazar', scholars debated whether a simple principle can lead to the desired object. Sanusi addresses an objection, explains the condition for transition, and clarifies why this process is not rule-governed. He also distinguishes the craft's contribution in deriving suitability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the passage: the objection that if the simple meaning is already obtained, the desired is already known, and if not, it cannot define. Then read Sanusi's answer: its entailment is conditional on intentional recall.
The key is the phrase 'مخطراً بالبال ملتفتاً إليه قصداً' (brought to mind deliberately).
2
Understand why it is unregulated
Read the second part: simple meanings vary by person, custom, and habit, so there is no universal rule for their transition.
This shows the definition is context-dependent.
3
Grasp the craft's role
Read the third part: the craft (sina'a) contributes only to deriving suitability, not the form. Most craft issues deal with form.
The craft's involvement is limited to finding suitable simple principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (مستلزم للانتقال إلخ) فإن قيل: ذلك المعنى البسيط إن كان حاصلاً يكون المطلوب حاصلاً لاستلزامه الانتقال إليه، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً لا يمكن التعريف به، قلت: استلزامه الانتقال إنما هو على تقدير كونه مخطراً بالبال ملتفتاً إليه قصداً، فيجوز أن يكون حاصلا بالتبع فإذا أخطر استلزم الانتقال . قوله : (لم ينضبط إلخ) لأن المعاني البسيطة التي تستلزم الانتقال إلى أخرى، تختلف بحسب اختلاف الأشخاص والعرف والعادات وليس له ضابط يعرف به ذلك . قوله : ( للصناعة إلخ) إذ مدخليتها فيه إنما هو باعتبار استفادة المناسبة للمطلوب، دون الصورة وأكثر مسائل الصناعة تتعلق بالصورة.
His statement: '(It is) necessitating transition...' etc. — For it may be said: that simple meaning, if it is already obtained, then the desired (al-matloob) is already obtained because it entails transition to it, and if it is not obtained, it cannot be used for definition. I say: its entailing transition is only on the condition that it is brought to mind deliberately and intentionally; it is possible that it is obtained accidentally, and when it is brought to mind, it then entails transition. His statement: 'It is not regulated...' etc. — because simple meanings that entail transition to others differ according to differences of persons, custom, and habits, and there is no rule by which that can be known. His statement: 'For the craft (al-sina‘a)...' etc. — because its involvement in it is only by way of deriving suitability for the desired, not the form; and most issues of the craft pertain to form.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of 'nazar' using a simple principle (mabda' basit). An objection is raised: if the simple meaning is already present, the desired is already present; if not present, it cannot define. The answer is that the entailment of transition is conditional on the simple meaning being intentionally considered (mukhtaran bi al-bal, multafatan ilayhi qasdan); it may be possessed accidentally (bi al-taba‘), and when it is brought to mind, transition follows. The commentator then explains why this is not regulated: simple meanings vary by person, custom, and habit, with no rule (dabit). Finally, the involvement of the craft (al-sina‘a) in this is only in deriving suitability (munasaba) for the desired, not the form (sura), whereas most issues of the craft deal with form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think a simple meaning cannot define because if the learner already has it, the definition is unnecessary, and if not, it cannot teach.
Correction: The simple meaning can be possessed accidentally; when brought to mind intentionally, it does lead to the desired object.

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a simple meaning entail transition to the desired object?
A) It is already obtained.
B) It is brought to mind deliberately and intentionally.
C) It is part of a compound definition.
D) It is universally known.

Key Takeaway
A simple principle defines when intentionally recalled, and its use is context-dependent without fixed rules; the craft only aids in finding suitability.

If defining 'reflection' (nazar) requires deliberate recall, what does that mean for your own reflection on God's signs? Has your contemplation been passive? Choose one verse of the Quran to intentionally bring to mind before you sleep and let it define your night.

The Simple Meaning Definition of Nazar and Its Rejection
The author presents the simple meaning definition of nazar and its rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians rejected the simplest definition of nazar (speculative reasoning) as a mere 'simple meaning' and what they adopted instead.

Core Idea
The simple definition of nazar was rejected because it could not be standardized.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational concept of nazar, or speculative reasoning. Theologians initially considered defining it as a simple meaning that automatically leads from premise to conclusion. However, they found this definition problematic because it lacked the precision and structure needed for a rigorous discipline. We will examine why this definition was set aside and what alternatives were proposed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the basic argument. Notice the key terms like معنى بسيط and انضباط.
2
Identify the simple meaning definition
The text defines nazar as a principle that is a simple meaning necessitating transition without need for context. Reflect on what this means.
3
Understand the reasons for rejection
The passage states two reasons: it was not regulated like compound definitions, and industry/choice had little role. Consider why these are problematic.
4
Examine the alternatives
The text mentions two alternative definitions: 'acquiring a thing' and 'arranging matters'. Compare them with the original definition.
These come from later scholars who found the original problem difficult.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why defining nazar is crucial for kalam. How does this discussion affect theological methodology?
This shows the importance of precision in theological terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبدأ الذي هو معنى بسيط مستلزم للانتقال إلى المطلوب من غير حاجة إلى قرينة إلا أنه لم ينضبط انضباط التعريف بالمعاني المركبة، ولم يكن أيضاً للصناعة وللاختيار فيه مزيد مدخل فلم يلتفتوا إليه وخصوا حد النظر بما هو المعتبر منه وهذا تحقيق ما نقله من ابن سينا ومنهم من استصعب الإشكال فغير تعريف النظر إلى أنه تحصيل أمر، أو ترتيب أمور
The principle which is a simple meaning necessitating transition to the desired [conclusion] without need for a context, except that it was not regulated in the manner of definitions by compound meanings, nor did industry or choice have much input into it, so they did not attend to it and they restricted the definition of nazar to that which is considered from it, and this is the verification of what was transmitted from Ibn Sina, and among them some found the problem difficult and changed the definition of nazar to 'acquiring a thing' or 'arranging matters'.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (speculative reasoning) as a simple meaning that necessarily leads from one idea to another without external context. However, this simple meaning could not be standardized like compound definitions because it varies by person and custom. Also, technical discipline (صناعة) and personal choice have little role in it. Therefore, scholars set it aside and instead defined nazar as the verified concept (المعتبر) derived from Ibn Sina. Some scholars, finding the problem difficult, redefined nazar as 'acquiring a thing' or 'arranging matters.' This shows the evolution of the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple meaning definition was rejected because it was unnecessary or invalid.
Correction: It was rejected because it could not be regulated like compound definitions, and it lacked the structure needed for a rigorous discipline.

Checkpoint

A) It required too many premises.
B) It could not be regulated like compound definitions.
C) It was based on Ibn Sina's opinion.
D) It did not lead to the desired conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The simple meaning definition of nazar was rejected because it could not be standardized, leading to adoption of a more structured concept from Ibn Sina and later redefinitions.

Objection and Answer on Simple Meaning Definition
The author gives an objection and answer to the simple meaning definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the simple meaning definition against the objection of circularity by distinguishing between implicit and explicit presence.

Core Idea
A definition's simple meaning works implicitly even when not consciously attended to.

This passage presents a key objection to defining something by a simple meaning: either the meaning is already known (making definition pointless) or it is unknown (making definition impossible). The author offers a nuanced answer based on how we attend to meanings. Understanding this rebuttal is crucial for grasping how definitions function in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice the dilemma: if the simple meaning is already obtained, the definition is unnecessary; if not obtained, definition is impossible.
The objection uses 'then the desired is obtained' meaning the conclusion is already reached.
2
Understand the two horns
Identify the two cases: obtained vs. not obtained. Reflect on why each seems to invalidate the definition.
3
Read the author's answer
Read the answer: 'I say: its necessitation of transition is only on the assumption of it being brought to mind...'
The key phrase is 'mukhtiran bil-bal' (brought to mind).
4
Note the key terms
Learn the terms: mustalzim lil-intiqal (necessitating transition), mukhtir bil-bal (brought to mind), bil-tabi' (secondarily/implicitly).
These terms are central to the argument.
5
Reflect on the solution
The answer shows that the simple meaning can be present implicitly. When it is consciously attended to, it leads to the conclusion. So the definition is valid.
This is a subtle but powerful defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (مستلزم للانتقال إلخ) فإن قيل: ذلك المعنى البسيط إن كان حاصلاً يكون المطلوب حاصلاً لاستلزامه الانتقال إليه، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً لا يمكن التعريف به، قلت: استلزامه الانتقال إنما هو على تقدير كونه مخطراً بالبال ملتفتاً إليه قصداً، فيجوز أن يكون حاصلا بالتبع فإذا أخطر استلزم الانتقال .
His statement: (necessitating transition etc.). For if it is said: that simple meaning, if it is obtained, then the desired is obtained because it necessitates transition to it, and if it is not obtained, definition by it is not possible. I say: its necessitation of transition is only on the assumption of it being brought to mind, intentionally attended to. So it may be obtained secondarily, and when brought to mind, it necessitates transition.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objection: If the simple meaning is already present in the mind, then the conclusion is already known, so definition is unnecessary. If it is not present, you cannot define by it. The answer: The simple meaning's necessitation of transition only occurs when it is consciously brought to mind. It may be present implicitly (بالتبع), and when thought of, it leads to the conclusion. So the definition works.

Key Terms

مخطر بالبال
بالتبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer concedes the objection by admitting that the simple meaning must be present, so the definition is still either unnecessary or impossible.
Correction: The answer does not concede; it shows that the simple meaning can be implicitly present and only when consciously attended does it lead to the conclusion. This saves the definition.

Checkpoint

A) He accepts the objection.
B) He says the simple meaning is only definitional when consciously considered.
C) He says the simple meaning is always known.
D) He redefines the term.

Key Takeaway
The simple meaning definition is saved by noting that the meaning can be implicitly present and only leads to transition when consciously attended to.
